<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<p><i>Falco buteo</i> <span style="font-size:85%;">Linnaeus,&#160;1758</span>
</p>
</td></tr>

</tbody></table>
<p>The <b>common buzzard</b> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>) is a medium-to-large <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a> which has a large range. A member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Buteo" title="Buteo">Buteo</a></i>, it is a member of the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>. The species lives in most of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and extends its breeding range across the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a> as far as the Russian Far East , northwestern China (Tien Shan) and northwestern Mongolia<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Over much of its range, it is a year-round resident. However, buzzards from the colder parts of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> as well as those that breed in the eastern part of their range typically migrate south for the northern winter, many culminating their journey as far as <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bildstein_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bildstein-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The common buzzard is an opportunistic predator that can take a wide variety of prey, but it feeds mostly on small <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>. It typically hunts from a perch.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Like most accipitrid birds of prey, it builds a nest, typically in trees in this species, and is a devoted parent to a relatively small brood of young.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The common buzzard appears to be the most common diurnal raptor in Europe, as estimates of its total global population run well into the millions.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BirdlifeEurope_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdlifeEurope-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">formal description</a> of the common buzzard was by the Swedish naturalist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1758 in the <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">tenth edition</a> of his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i> under the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial name</a> <i>Falco buteo</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Linnaeus1758_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linnaeus1758-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The genus <i>Buteo</i> was introduced by the French naturalist <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Germain_de_Lac%C3%A9p%C3%A8de" title="Bernard Germain de Lacépède">Bernard Germain de Lacépède</a> in 1799 by <a href="/wiki/Tautonymy" class="mw-redirect" title="Tautonymy">tautonymy</a> with the specific name of this species.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The word <i>buteo</i> is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for a buzzard.<sup id="cite_ref-job90_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job90-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It should not be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Turkey_vulture" title="Turkey vulture">Turkey vulture</a>, which is sometimes called a buzzard in American English.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Buteoninae" title="Buteoninae">Buteoninae</a> subfamily originated from and is most diversified in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, with occasional broader radiations that led to common buzzards and other Eurasian and African buzzards. The common buzzard is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Buteo" title="Buteo">Buteo</a></i>, a group of medium-sized raptors with robust bodies and broad wings. The <i>Buteo</i> species of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> are usually commonly referred to as "buzzards" while those in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> are called hawks. Under current classification, the genus includes approximately 28 species, the second most diverse of all extant accipitrid genera behind only <i><a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiter</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Riesing_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riesing-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> DNA testing shows that the common buzzard is fairly closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a> (<i>Buteo jamaicensis</i>) of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, which occupies a similar <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a> to the buzzard in that continent. The two species may belong to the same <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Riesing_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riesing-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Two buzzards in Africa are likely closely related to the common buzzard based on genetic materials, the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_buzzard" title="Mountain buzzard">mountain</a> (<i>Buteo oreophilus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Forest_buzzard" title="Forest buzzard">forest buzzards</a> (<i>Buteo trizonatus</i>), to the point where it has been questioned whether they are sufficiently distinct to qualify as full species. However, the distinctiveness of these African buzzards has generally been supported.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haring_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haring-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Siegfried_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siegfried-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Genetic studies have further indicated that the modern buzzards of Eurasia and Africa are a relatively young group, showing that they diverged at about 300,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Clouet_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clouet-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, fossils dating earlier than 5 million year old (the late <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> period) showed <i>Buteo</i> species were present in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> much earlier than that would imply, although it cannot be stated to a certainty that these would’ve been related to the extant buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies_and_species_splits">Subspecies and species splits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Subspecies and species splits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1706" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_-Netherlands-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical intermediate brown buzzard of the nominate race (<i>B. b. buteo</i>) in the Netherlands</div></div></div>
<p>Some 16 subspecies have been described in the past and up to 11 are often considered valid, although some authorities accept as few as seven.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Common buzzard subspecies fall into two groups.
</p><p>The western <i>buteo</i> group is mainly resident or short-distance <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrants</a> and includes:
</p>
<ul><li><i>B. b. buteo</i>: Ranges in Europe from the Atlantic islands, the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Madeira_Island" title="Madeira Island">Madeira Island</a>, whose population was once considered a separate race, <i>B. b. harterti</i>) more or less continuously throughout Europe to <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This highly individually variable race is described below. This is a relatively large and bulky race of buzzard. In males, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> ranges from 350 to 418&#160;mm (13.8 to 16.5&#160;in) and the tail from 194 to 223&#160;mm (7.6 to 8.8&#160;in). In comparison, the larger female has a wing chord measuring 374 to 432&#160;mm (14.7 to 17.0&#160;in) and tail length of 193 to 236&#160;mm (7.6 to 9.3&#160;in). In both sexes, the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> measures 69 to 83&#160;mm (2.7 to 3.3&#160;in) in length.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> As illustrated by average body mass, sizes in the nominate race of common buzzard seem to confirm to <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a>, increasing to the north and decreasing closer to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In southern <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, the mean weight of males was reportedly 740&#160;g (1.63&#160;lb), while that of females was 1,100&#160;g (2.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Site_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Site-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> British buzzards were of intermediate size, 214 males averaging 781&#160;g (1.722&#160;lb) and 261 females averaging 969&#160;g (2.136&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC2_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Birds to the south in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> were smaller, averaging 662&#160;g (1.459&#160;lb) in 22 males and 800&#160;g (1.8&#160;lb) in 30 females.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Cramp and Simmons (1980) listed the mean body mass overall of nominate buzzards in Europe overall as 828&#160;g (1.825&#160;lb) in males and 1,052&#160;g (2.319&#160;lb) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4252" data-file-height="3307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_rotschildi_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Buteo buteo rothschildi</i> – <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><i>B. b. rothschildi</i>: This proposed race is native to the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> islands. It is generally considered a valid subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This race differs from a typical intermediate of the nominate in being a darker, colder brown both above and below, closer to the darker individuals of the nominate. It averages smaller than most nominate buzzards. The <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> of males ranges from 343 to 365&#160;mm (13.5 to 14.4&#160;in) while that of females ranges from 362 to 393&#160;mm (14.3 to 15.5&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. insularum</i>: This race lives in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>. Not all authorities consider this race suitably distinct, but others advocate it be retained as a full subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It is typically of richer brown above and more heavily streaked below compared to nominate birds. It is similar in size to <i>B. b. rothschildi</i> and averages slightly smaller than the nominate race. Males have a reported <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> of 352 to 390&#160;mm (13.9 to 15.4&#160;in) and females have a wing chord of 370 to 394&#160;mm (14.6 to 15.5&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. arrigonii</i>: This race inhabits the islands of <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>. It is generally considered a valid subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The upper-side of these buzzards is an intermediate brown with very heavy streaking below, often covering the belly whereas most nominate buzzards show a whitish area the middle of the belly. Like most other insular races, this one is relatively small. Males possess a <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> of 343 to 382&#160;mm (13.5 to 15.0&#160;in) while females have a wing chord of 353 to 390&#160;mm (13.9 to 15.4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_(Steppe)_Buzzard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Common_%28Steppe%29_Buzzard.jpg/220px-Common_%28Steppe%29_Buzzard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Common_%28Steppe%29_Buzzard.jpg/330px-Common_%28Steppe%29_Buzzard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Common_%28Steppe%29_Buzzard.jpg/440px-Common_%28Steppe%29_Buzzard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="1319" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_(Steppe)_Buzzard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The steppe race of the common buzzard (<i>B. b. vulpinus</i>) is usually distinguished from the nominate race (<i>B. b. buteo</i>) by being more rufous in hue (and more polymorphic)</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern <i>vulpinus</i> group includes:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4252" data-file-height="3307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_vulpinus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.89.7.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Buteo buteo vulpinus</i> – <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><i>B. b. vulpinus</i>: The <b>steppe buzzard</b> breeds as far west as eastern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, in the southern two-thirds of <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, much of <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, eastward to the northern <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">Caucacus</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, much of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Altai_Republic" title="Altai Republic">Altai</a> and south-central <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tien_Shan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tien Shan">Tien Shan</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>. <i>B. b. vulpinus</i> is a long-distance <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrant</a>. It winters largely in much of eastern and southern <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. Less frequently and often very discontinuously, steppe buzzards winter in the southern peninsulas of Europe, <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> and southwestern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> in addition to some parts of southeastern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>. In the open country favoured on the wintering grounds, steppe buzzards are often seen perched on roadside telephone poles.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> It at one time was considered a separate species due to differences in size, form, colouring and behaviour (especially in regards to migratory behaviour) but is genetically indistinct from nominate buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kruckenhauser_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruckenhauser-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the steppe buzzard engages in extensive interbreeding with the nominate race, mudding typical characteristics of both races. The zone of integration runs from <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> through <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, including any part of the overlapping ranges in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>. At times, the fertile hybrids of these two races have been erroneously proposed as races such as <i>B. b. intermedius</i> or <i>B. b. zimmermannae</i>. Intergrade buzzards are commonest where the grey-brown type of pale morphs of <i>vulpinus</i> are predominant.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Steppe buzzards are usually distinctly smaller, with relatively longer wings and tail for their size, and thus often appear swifter and more agile in flight than nominate buzzards, whose wing beats can look slower and clumsier. Typically, their length is around 45 to 50&#160;cm (18 to 20&#160;in), while wingspan of males average 113&#160;cm (44&#160;in) and females average 122.7&#160;cm (48&#160;in). The <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> is 335 to 377&#160;mm (13.2 to 14.8&#160;in) in males and 358 to 397&#160;mm (14.1 to 15.6&#160;in) in females. Tail length is 170 to 200&#160;mm (6.7 to 7.9&#160;in) in males and 175 to 209&#160;mm (6.9 to 8.2&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Weights of birds from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> can reportedly range from 560 to 675&#160;g (1.235 to 1.488&#160;lb) in males and 710 to 1,180&#160;g (1.57 to 2.60&#160;lb) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Weights of migrant birds appear to be lower than at other times of year for steppe buzzards. Two surveys of migrant buzzards during their huge spring movement in <a href="/wiki/Eilat" title="Eilat">Eilat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> showed 420 birds averaged 579&#160;g (1.276&#160;lb) and 882 birds averaged 578&#160;g (1.274&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Gorney_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorney-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In comparison, weights of wintering steppe buzzards was higher, averaging 725&#160;g (1.598&#160;lb) in 35 birds in the former <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">Transvaal</a> (<a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>) and 739&#160;g (1.629&#160;lb) in 160 birds in the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a>. Weights of birds from <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> were similar.<sup id="cite_ref-Gorney_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorney-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. menetriesi</i>: This race is found in southern <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> to northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and possibly into <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(country)" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkey (country)">Turkey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This race has traditionally been listed as a resident race, but some sources consider it a migrant to eastern and southern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Compared to the overlapping steppe buzzard subspecies, it is larger (roughly intermediate between the nominate race and <i>vulpinus</i>) and is duller in overall colour, being sandy below rather than rufous and lacking the bright rufous on the tail. <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">Wing chord</a> is 351 to 397&#160;mm (13.8 to 15.6&#160;in) in males and 372 to 413&#160;mm (14.6 to 16.3&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kruckenhauser_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruckenhauser-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>At one time, races of the common buzzard were thought to range as far in Asia as a breeding bird well into the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and as far east as northeastern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">Sea of Okhotsk</a>, and all the islands of the <a href="/wiki/Kurile_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurile Islands">Kurile Islands</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, despite both the Himalayan and eastern birds showing a natural gap in distribution from the next nearest breeding common buzzard. However, DNA testing has revealed that the buzzards of these populations probably belong to different species. Most authorities now accept these buzzards as full species: the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_buzzard" title="Eastern buzzard">eastern buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo japonicus</i>; with three subspecies of its own) and the <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_buzzard" title="Himalayan buzzard">Himalayan buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo refectus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Kruckenhauser_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruckenhauser-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Buzzards found on the islands of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a> off of the coast of western Africa, once referred to as the subspecies <i>B. b. bannermani</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Socotra" title="Socotra">Socotra Island</a> off of the northern peninsula of <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, once referred to as the rarely recognized subspecies <i>B. b. socotrae</i>, are now generally thought not to belong to the common buzzard. DNA testing has indicated that these insular buzzards are actually more closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo rufinus</i>) than to the common buzzard. Subsequently, some researchers have advocated full species status for the Cape Verde population, but the placement of these buzzards is generally deemed unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clouet_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clouet-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_2_(Marek_Szczepanek).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Buteo_buteo_2_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_2_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Buteo_buteo_2_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_2_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Buteo_buteo_2_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_2_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="436" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_2_(Marek_Szczepanek).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dark individual from Europe</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard is a medium-sized raptor that is highly variable in plumage. Most buzzards are distinctly round headed with a somewhat slender bill, relatively long wings that either reach or fall slightly short of the tail tip when perched, a fairly short tail, and somewhat short and mainly bare tarsi. They can appear fairly compact in overall appearance but may also appear large relative to other commoner raptorial birds such as <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_kestrel" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian kestrel">kestrels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">sparrowhawks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The common buzzard measures between 40 and 58&#160;cm (16 and 23&#160;in) in length with a 109–140&#160;cm (43–55&#160;in) wingspan.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Females average about 2–7% larger than males linearly and weigh about 15% more. Body mass can show considerable variation. Buzzards from <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> alone can vary from 427 to 1,183&#160;g (0.941 to 2.608&#160;lb) in males, while females there can range from 486 to 1,370&#160;g (1.071 to 3.020&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-delHoyo_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delHoyo-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_4_(Marek_Szczepanek).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Buteo_buteo_4_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_4_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Buteo_buteo_4_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_4_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Buteo_buteo_4_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_4_%28Marek_Szczepanek%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="433" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_4_(Marek_Szczepanek).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pale individual in Europe</div></div></div>
<p>In Europe, most typical buzzards are dark brown above and on the upperside of the head and mantle, but can become paler and warmer brown with worn plumage. The flight feathers on perched European buzzards are always brown in the nominate subspecies (<i>B. b. buteo</i>). Usually the tail will usually be narrowly barred grey-brown and dark brown with a pale tip and a broad dark subterminal band but the tail in palest birds can show a varying amount a white and reduced subterminal band or even appear almost all white. In European buzzards, the underside coloring can be variable but most typically show a brown-streaked white throat with a somewhat darker chest. A pale U across breast is often present; followed by a pale line running down the belly which separates the dark areas on breast-side and flanks. These pale areas tend to have highly variable markings that tend to form irregular bars. Juvenile buzzards are quite similar to adult in the nominate race, being best told apart by having a paler eye, a narrower subterminal band on the tail and underside markings that appear as streaks rather than bars. Furthermore, juveniles may show variable creamy to rufous fringes to upperwing coverts but these also may not be present. Seen from below in flight, buzzards in Europe typically have a dark trailing edge to the wings. If seen from above, one of the best marks is their broad dark subterminal tail band. Flight feathers of typical European buzzards are largely greyish, the aforementioned dark wing linings at front with contrasting paler band along the median coverts. In flight, paler individuals tend to show dark carpal patches that can appears as blackish arches or commas but these may be indistinct in darker individuals or can appear light brownish or faded in paler individuals. Juvenile nominate buzzards are best told apart from adults in flight by the lack of a distinct subterminal band (instead showing fairly even barring throughout) and below by having less sharp and brownish rather than blackish trailing wing edge. Juvenile buzzards show streaking paler parts of under wing and body showing rather than barring as do adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark2_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Beyond the typical mid-range brownish buzzard, birds in Europe can range from almost uniform black-brown above to mainly white. Extreme dark individuals may range from chocolate brown to blackish with almost no pale showing but a variable, faded U on the breast and with or without faint lighter brown throat streaks. Extreme pale birds are largely whitish with variable widely spaced streaks or arrowheads of light brown about the mid-chest and flanks and may or may not show dark feather-centres on the head, wing-coverts and sometimes all but part of mantle. Individuals can show nearly endless variation of colours and hues in between these extremes and the common buzzard is counted among the most variably plumage diurnal raptors for this reason.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> One study showed that this variation may actually be the result of diminished <a href="/wiki/Locus_(genetics)" title="Locus (genetics)">single-locus</a> genetic diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common-Buzzard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Common-Buzzard.jpg/220px-Common-Buzzard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Common-Buzzard.jpg/330px-Common-Buzzard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Common-Buzzard.jpg/440px-Common-Buzzard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="746" data-file-height="1085" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common-Buzzard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Although they can look compact when perched, buzzards may appear large and long-winged in flight.</div></div></div>
<p>Beyond the nominate form (<i>B. b. buteo</i>) that occupies most of the common buzzard's European range, a second main, widely distributed subspecies is known as the steppe buzzard (<i>B. b. vulpinus</i>). The steppe buzzard race shows three main colour morphs, each of which can be predominant in a region of breeding range. It is more distinctly <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphic</a> rather than just individually very variable like the nominate race. This may be because, unlike the nominate buzzard, the steppe buzzard is highly migratory. Polymorphism has been linked with migratory behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The most common type of steppe buzzard is the rufous morph which gives this subspecies its scientific name (<i>vulpes</i> is Latin for "fox"). This morph comprises a majority of birds seen in passage east of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>. Rufous morph buzzards are a paler grey-brown above than most nominate <i>B. b. buteo</i>. Compared to the nominate race, rufous <i>vulpinus</i> show a patterning not dissimilar but generally far more rufous-toned on head, the fringes to mantle wing coverts and, especially, on the tail and the underside. The head is grey-brown with rufous tinges usually while the tail is rufous and can vary from almost unmarked to thinly dark-barred with a subterminal band. The underside can be uniformly pale to dark rufous, barred heavily or lightly with rufous or with dusky barring, usually with darker individuals showing the U as in nominate but with a rufous hue. The pale morph of the steppe buzzard is commonest in the west of its subspecies range, predominantly seen in winter and migration at the various land bridge of the Mediterranean. As in the rufous morph, the pale morph <i>vulpinus</i> is grey-brown above but the tail is generally marked with thin dark bars and a subterminal band, only showing rufous near the tip. The underside in the pale morph is greyish-white with dark grey-brown or somewhat streaked head to chest and barred belly and chest, occasionally showing darker flanks that can be somewhat rufous. Dark morph <i>vulpinus</i> tend to be found in the east and southeast of the subspecies range and are easily outnumbered by rufous morph while largely using similar migration points. Dark morph individuals vary from grey-brown to much darker blackish-brown, and have a tail that is dark grey or somewhat mixed grey and rufous, is distinctly marked with dark barring and has a broad, black subterminal band. Dark morph <i>vulpinus</i> have a head and underside that is mostly uniform dark, from dark brown to blackish-brown to almost pure black. Rufous morph juveniles are often distinctly paler in ground colour (ranging even to creamy-grey) than adults with distinct barring below actually increased in pale morph type juvenile. Pale and rufous morph juveniles can only be distinguished from each other in extreme cases. Dark morph juveniles are more similar to adult dark morph <i>vulpinus</i> but often show a little whitish streaking below, and like all other races have lighter coloured eyes and more evenly barred tails than adults. Steppe buzzards tend to appear smaller and more agile in flight than nominate whose wing beats can look slower and clumsier. In flight, rufous morph <i>vulpinus</i> have their whole body and underwing varying from uniform to patterned rufous (if patterning present, it is variable, but can be on chest and often thighs, sometimes flanks, pale band across median coverts), while the under-tail usually paler rufous than above. Whitish flight feathers are more prominent than in nominate and more marked contrast with the bold dark brown band along the trailing edges. Markings of pale <i>vulpinus</i> as seen in flight are similar to rufous morph (such as paler wing markings) but more greyish both on wings and body. In dark morph <i>vulpinus</i> the broad black trailing edges and colour of body make whitish areas of inner wing stand out further with an often bolder and blacker carpal patch than in other morphs. As in nominate, juvenile <i>vulpinus</i> (rufous/pale) tend to have much less distinct trailing edges, general streaking on body and along median underwing coverts. Dark morph <i>vulpinus</i> resemble adult in flight more so than other morphs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark2_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Similar_species">Similar species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Similar species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forest_Buzzard,_Buteo_trizonatus,_at_Hangklip_Forest,_Makhado,_Limpopo_Province,_South_Africa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Forest_Buzzard%2C_Buteo_trizonatus%2C_at_Hangklip_Forest%2C_Makhado%2C_Limpopo_Province%2C_South_Africa.jpg/220px-Forest_Buzzard%2C_Buteo_trizonatus%2C_at_Hangklip_Forest%2C_Makhado%2C_Limpopo_Province%2C_South_Africa.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Forest_Buzzard%2C_Buteo_trizonatus%2C_at_Hangklip_Forest%2C_Makhado%2C_Limpopo_Province%2C_South_Africa.jpg/330px-Forest_Buzzard%2C_Buteo_trizonatus%2C_at_Hangklip_Forest%2C_Makhado%2C_Limpopo_Province%2C_South_Africa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Forest_Buzzard%2C_Buteo_trizonatus%2C_at_Hangklip_Forest%2C_Makhado%2C_Limpopo_Province%2C_South_Africa.jpg/440px-Forest_Buzzard%2C_Buteo_trizonatus%2C_at_Hangklip_Forest%2C_Makhado%2C_Limpopo_Province%2C_South_Africa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1474" data-file-height="1556" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forest_Buzzard,_Buteo_trizonatus,_at_Hangklip_Forest,_Makhado,_Limpopo_Province,_South_Africa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile <a href="/wiki/Forest_buzzard" title="Forest buzzard">forest buzzards</a> of Africa are extremely easy to mistake for juvenile common buzzards of the steppe race that come to winter in Africa.</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard is often confused with other raptors especially in flight or at a distance. Inexperienced and over-enthusiastic observers have even mistaken darker birds for the far larger and differently proportioned <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>) and also dark birds for <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harrier</a> (<i>Circus aeruginosus</i>) which also flies in a dihedral but is obviously relatively much longer and slenderer winged and tailed and with far different flying methods. Also buzzards may possibly be confused with dark or light morph <a href="/wiki/Booted_eagle" title="Booted eagle">booted eagles</a> (<i>Hieraeetus pennatus</i>), which are similar in size, but the eagle flies on level, parallel-edged wings which usually appear broader, has a longer squarer tail, with no carpal patch in pale birds and all dark flight feathers but for whitish wedge on inner primaries in dark morph ones. Pale individuals are sometimes also mistaken with pale morph <a href="/wiki/Short-toed_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-toed eagle">short-toed eagles</a> (<i>Circaetus gallicus</i>) which are much larger with a considerably bigger head, longer wings (which are usually held evenly in flight rather than in a dihedral) and paler underwing lacking any carpal patch or dark wing lining.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark2_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> More serious identification concerns lie in other <i>Buteo</i> species and in flight with <a href="/wiki/Pernis_(bird)" title="Pernis (bird)">honey buzzards</a>, which are quite different looking when seen perched at close range. The <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">European honey buzzard</a> (<i>Pernis apivorus</i>) is thought in engage in mimicry of more powerful raptors, in particular, juveniles may mimic the plumage of the more powerful common buzzard.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> While less individually variable in Europe, the honey buzzard is more extensive polymorphic on underparts than even the common buzzard. The most common morph of the adult European honey buzzard is heavily and rufous barred on the underside, quite different from the common buzzard, however the brownish juvenile much more resembles an intermediate common buzzard. Honey buzzards flap with distinctively slower and more even wing beats than common buzzard. The wings are also lifted higher on each upstroke, creating a more regular and mechanical effect, furthermore their wings are held slightly arched when soaring but not in a V. On the honey buzzard, the head appears smaller, the body thinner, the tail longer and the wings narrower and more parallel edged. The steppe buzzard race is particularly often mistaken for juvenile European honey buzzards, to the point where early observers of raptor migration in Israel considered distant individuals indistinguishable. However, when compared to a steppe buzzard, the honey buzzard has distinctly darker secondaries on the underwing with fewer and broader bars and more extensive black wing-tips (whole fingers) contrasting with a less extensively pale hand.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Found in the same range as the steppe buzzard in some parts of southern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> as well as (with wintering steppes) in southwestern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Oriental_honey_buzzard" class="mw-redirect" title="Oriental honey buzzard">Oriental honey buzzard</a> (<i>Pernis ptilorhynchus</i>) is larger than both the European honey buzzard and the common buzzard. The oriental species is with more similar in body plan to common buzzards, being relatively broader winged, shorter tailed and more amply-headed (though the head is still relatively small) relative to the European honey buzzard, but all plumages lack carpal patches.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In much of Europe, the common buzzard is the only type of buzzard. However, the subarctic breeding <a href="/wiki/Rough-legged_buzzard" title="Rough-legged buzzard">rough-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo lagopus</i>) comes down to occupy much of the northern part of the continent during winter in the same haunts as the common buzzard. However, the <a href="/wiki/Rough-legged_buzzard" title="Rough-legged buzzard">rough-legged buzzard</a> is typically larger and distinctly longer-winged with feathered legs, as well as having a white based tail with a broad subterminal band. Rough-legged buzzards have slower wing beats and hover far more frequently than do common buzzards. The carpal patch marking on the under-wing are also bolder and blacker on all paler forms of rough-legged hawk. Many pale morph rough-legged buzzards have a bold, blackish band across the belly against contrasting paler feathers, a feature which rarely appears in individual common buzzard. Usually the face also appears somewhat whitish in most pale morphs of rough-legged buzzards, which is true of only extremely pale common buzzards. Dark morph rough-legged buzzards are usually distinctly darker (ranging to almost blackish) than even extreme dark individuals of common buzzards in Europe and still have the distinct white-based tail and broad subterminal band of other roughlegs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">eastern Europe</a> and much of the Asian range of common buzzards, the <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo rufinus</i>) may live alongside the common species. As in the steppe buzzard race, the long-legged buzzard has three main colour morphs that are more or less similar in hue. In both the steppe buzzard race and long-legged buzzard, the main colour is overall fairly rufous. More so than steppe buzzards, long-legged buzzards tend to have a distinctly paler head and neck compared to other feathers, and, more distinctly, a normally unbarred tail. Furthermore, the long-legged buzzard is usually a rather larger bird, often considered fairly <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a>-like in appearance (although it does appear gracile and small-billed even compared to smaller true eagles), an effect enhanced by its longer tarsi, somewhat longer neck and relatively elongated wings. The flight style of the latter species is deeper, slower and more aquiline, with much more frequent hovering, showing a more protruding head and a slightly higher V held in a soar. The smaller <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabian</a> race of long-legged buzzard (<i>B. r. cirtensis</i>) is more similar in size and nearly all colour characteristics to steppe buzzard, extending to the heavily streaked juvenile plumage, in some cases such birds can be distinguished only by their proportions and flight patterns which remain unchanged.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Hybridization with the latter race (<i>B. r. cirtensis</i>) and nominate common buzzards has been observed in the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a>, a few such birds have been reported potentially in the southern Mediterranean due to mutually encroaching ranges, which are blurring possibly due to climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wintering steppe buzzards may live alongside <a href="/wiki/Mountain_buzzard" title="Mountain buzzard">mountain buzzards</a> and especially with <a href="/wiki/Forest_buzzard" title="Forest buzzard">forest buzzard</a> while wintering in Africa. The juveniles of steppe and forest buzzards are more or less indistinguishable and only told apart by proportions and flight style, the latter species being smaller, more compact, having a smaller bill, shorter legs and shorter and thinner wings than a steppe buzzard. However, size is not diagnostic unless side by side as the two buzzards overlap in this regard. Most reliable are the species wing proportions and their flight actions. Forest buzzard have more flexible wing beats interspersed with glides, additionally soaring on flatter wings and apparently never engage in hovering. Adult forest buzzards compared to the typical adult steppe buzzard (rufous morph) are also similar, but the forest typically has a whiter underside, sometimes mostly plain white, usually with heavy blotches or drop-shaped marks on abdomen, with barring on thighs, more narrow tear-shaped on chest and more spotted on leading edges of underwing, usually lacking marking on the white U across chest (which is otherwise similar but usually broader than that of <i>vulpinus</i>). In comparison, the mountain buzzard, which is more similar in size to the steppe buzzard and slightly larger than the forest buzzard, is usually duller brown above than a steppe buzzard and is more whitish below with distinctive heavy brown blotches from breasts to the belly, flanks and wing linings while juvenile mountain buzzard is buffy below with smaller and streakier markings. The steppe buzzard when compared to another African species, the <a href="/wiki/Red-necked_buzzard" title="Red-necked buzzard">red-necked buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo auguralis</i>), which has red tail similar to <i>vulpinus</i>, is distinct in all other plumage aspects despite their similar size. The latter buzzard has a streaky rufous head and is white below with a contrasting bold dark chest in adult plumage and, in juvenile plumage, has heavy, dark blotches on the chest and flanks with pale wing-linings. <a href="/wiki/Jackal_buzzard" title="Jackal buzzard">Jackal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augur_buzzard" title="Augur buzzard">augur buzzards</a> (<i>Buteo rufofuscus</i> &amp; <i>augur</i>), also both rufous on the tail, are larger and bulkier than steppe buzzards and have several distinctive plumage characteristics, most notably both having their own striking, contrasting patterns of black-brown, rufous and cream.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_1_(Lukasz_Lukasik).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Buteo_buteo_1_%28Lukasz_Lukasik%29.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_1_%28Lukasz_Lukasik%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Buteo_buteo_1_%28Lukasz_Lukasik%29.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_1_%28Lukasz_Lukasik%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Buteo_buteo_1_%28Lukasz_Lukasik%29.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_1_%28Lukasz_Lukasik%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_1_(Lukasz_Lukasik).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Common buzzard often inhabit the interface of woods and open areas.</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard is found throughout several islands in the eastern Atlantic islands, including the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> and almost throughout Europe. It is today found in Ireland and in nearly every part of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. In mainland Europe, remarkably, there are no substantial gaps without breeding common buzzards from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, though are present mainly only in the breeding season in much of the eastern half of the latter three countries. They are also present in all larger <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> islands such as <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>. Further north in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, they are found mainly in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (though also some points in southwestern Norway close to the coast and one section north of <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a>), just over the southern half of <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and hugging over the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> where they live as a breeding species over nearly two-thirds of the land. The common buzzard reaches its northern limits as a breeder in far eastern Finland and over the border to <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>, continuing as a breeder over to the narrowest straits of the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> and nearly to the <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a>. In these northern quarters, the common buzzard is present typically only in summer but is a year-around resident of a hearty bit of southern Sweden and some of southern Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BirdlifeEurope_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdlifeEurope-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Outside of Europe, it is a resident of northern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> (largely close to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>) otherwise occurring mainly as a passage migrant or winter visitor in the remainder of Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, sporadically but not rarely in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (largely hugging the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>) to northern <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Further north though its absent from either side of the northern Caspian Sea, the common buzzard is found in much of western Russia (though exclusively as a breeder) including all of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Federal_District" title="Central Federal District">Central Federal District</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Volga_Federal_District" title="Volga Federal District">Volga Federal District</a>, all but the northernmost parts of the <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Federal_District" title="Northwestern Federal District">Northwestern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ural_Federal_District" title="Ural Federal District">Ural Federal Districts</a> and nearly the southern half of the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Federal_District" title="Siberian Federal District">Siberian Federal District</a>, its farthest easterly occurrence as a breeder. It also found in northern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, far northwestern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tien_Shan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tien Shan">Tien Shan</a>) and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Non-breeding populations occur, either as migrants or wintering birds, in southwestern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (northeastern), northern <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> (and far northwestern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>), northern <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, near the coasts of <a href="/wiki/The_Gambia" title="The Gambia">The Gambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> and far southwestern <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> (and bordering <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a>). In eastern and central Africa, it is found in winter from southeastern <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, about two-thirds of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meheretu2019_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meheretu2019-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> much of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> (though apparently absent from the northeast and northwest), <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, southern and eastern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, and more or less the entirety of southern Africa from <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> across to <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> down the remainder of the continent (but for an apparent gap along the coast from southwestern <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> to northwestern <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The common buzzard generally inhabits the interface of <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a> and open grounds; most typically the species lives in <a href="/wiki/Woodland_edge" title="Woodland edge">forest edge</a>, small woods or <a href="/wiki/Windbreak" title="Windbreak">shelterbelts</a> with adjacent <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arables</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">farmland</a>. It acquits to open <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moorland</a> as long as there is some trees. The woods they inhabit may be <a href="/wiki/Temperate_coniferous_forest" title="Temperate coniferous forest">coniferous</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperate_broad-leafed_or_mixed_forest&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Temperate broad-leafed or mixed forest (page does not exist)">temperate broad-leafed or mixed forests</a> with occasional preferences for the local dominant tree. It is absent from treeless <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> and sporadic or rare in treeless <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> but can migrate through these, and may be found to some extent in both in <a href="/wiki/Montane_ecosystems" title="Montane ecosystems">mountainous</a> or flat country.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Buzzards in well-wooded areas of eastern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> largely used large, mature stands of trees that were more humid, richer and denser than prevalent in surrounding area, but showed preference for those within 30 to 90&#160;m (98 to 295&#160;ft) of openings.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Mostly resident buzzards live in lowlands and <a href="/wiki/Foothill" class="mw-redirect" title="Foothill">foothills</a>, but they can live in timbered ridges and uplands as well as <a href="/wiki/Rocky_shore" title="Rocky shore">rocky coasts</a>, sometimes nesting on cliff ledges rather than trees. Buzzards may live from sea level to elevations of 2,000&#160;m (6,600&#160;ft), breeding mostly below 1,000&#160;m (3,300&#160;ft) but they can winter to an elevation of 2,500&#160;m (8,200&#160;ft) and migrates easily to 4,500&#160;m (14,800&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the mountainous Italian <a href="/wiki/Apennines" class="mw-redirect" title="Apennines">Apennines</a>, buzzard nests were at a mean elevation of 1,399&#160;m (4,590&#160;ft) and were, relative to the surrounding area, further from human developed areas (i.e. roads) and nearer to valley bottoms in rugged, irregularly topographed places, especially ones that faced northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Common buzzards are fairly adaptable to agricultural lands but will show can show regional declines in apparent response to agriculture. Changes to more extensive agricultural practices were shown to reduce buzzard populations in western <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> where reduction of “<a href="/wiki/Hedge" title="Hedge">hedgerows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodlot" title="Woodlot">woodlots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a> areas" caused a decline of buzzards and in <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> where more extensive grazing by free-range <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a> led to declines of buzzards, probably largely due to the seeming reduction of small <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammal</a> populations there.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, urbanization seems to negatively affect buzzards, this species being generally less adaptable to urban areas than their New World counterparts, the <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a>. Although <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">peri-urban areas</a> can actually increase potential prey populations in a location, individual buzzard mortality, nest disturbances and nest <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat degradation</a> rises significantly in such areas.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rooney2_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_%281%29.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_%281%29.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_%281%29.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_-Scotland-8_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buzzards spend much of their day perched.</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard is a typical <i>Buteo</i> in much of its behaviour. It is most often seen either soaring at varying heights or perched prominently on tree tops, bare branches, telegraph poles, fence posts, rocks or ledges, or alternately well inside tree canopies. Buzzards will also stand and forage on the ground. In resident populations, it may spend more than half of its day inactively perched. Furthermore, it has been described a "sluggish and not very bold" bird of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> It is a gifted soarer once aloft and can do so for extended periods but can appear laborious and heavy in level flight, more so nominate buzzards than steppe buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Particularly in migration, as was recorded in the case of steppe buzzards' movement over <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, buzzards readily adjust their direction, tail and wing placement and flying height to adjust for the surrounding environment and wind conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Israeli migrant buzzards rarely soar all that high (maximum 1,000–2,000&#160;m (3,300–6,600&#160;ft) above ground) due to the lack of mountain ridges that in other areas typically produce flyways; however tail-winds are significant and allow birds to cover a mean of 9.8 metres per second (22 miles per hour).<sup id="cite_ref-Spaar_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spaar-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_(15626160697).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_%2815626160697%29.jpg/220px-Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_%2815626160697%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_%2815626160697%29.jpg/330px-Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_%2815626160697%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_%2815626160697%29.jpg/440px-Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_%2815626160697%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2517" data-file-height="1412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Israel._Steppe_Buzzard_(15626160697).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steppe buzzard migrating through <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, where buzzards have one of the largest raptor migrations in the world.</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard is aptly described as a partial migrant.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The autumn and spring movements of buzzards are subject to extensive variation, even down to the individual level, based on a region's food resources, competition (both from other buzzards and other predators), extent of human disturbance and weather conditions. Short distance movements are the norm for juveniles and some adults in autumn and winter, but more adults in central Europe and the British Isles remain on their year-around residence than do not.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wuczyński_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wuczyński-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Even for first year juvenile buzzards dispersal may not take them very far. In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, 96% of first-years moved in winter to less than 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) from their natal site.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Southwestern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> was recorded to be a fairly important wintering grounds for central European buzzards in early spring that apparently travelled from somewhat farther north, in winter average density was a locally high 2.12 individual per square kilometer.<sup id="cite_ref-Wuczyński_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wuczyński-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Habitat and prey availability seemed to be the primary drivers of habitat selection in fall for European buzzards. In northern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, buzzards were recorded to show preferences in fall for areas fairly distant from nesting site, with a large quantity of vole-holes and more widely dispersed perches.<sup id="cite_ref-Schindler_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schindler-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, the mean wintering density was 0.34 individual per square kilometer, and buzzards showed a preference for agricultural over forested areas.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Similar habitat preferences were recorded in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, where buzzard density was 0.334–0.539 individuals per square kilometer.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The nominate buzzards of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> are somewhat more strongly migratory than most central European populations. However, birds from <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> show some variation in migratory behaviours. A maximum of 41,000 individuals have been recorded at one of the main migration sites within southern Sweden in <a href="/wiki/Falsterbo" title="Falsterbo">Falsterbo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In southern Sweden, winter movements and migration was studied via observation of buzzard colour. White individuals were substantially more common in southern Sweden rather than further north in their Swedish range. The southern population migrates earlier than intermediate to dark buzzards, in both adults and juveniles. A larger proportion of juveniles than of adults migrate in the southern population. Especially adults in the southern population are resident to a higher degree than more northerly breeders.<sup id="cite_ref-Kjellen_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kjellen-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The entire population of the steppe buzzard is strongly migratory, covering substantial distances during migration. In no part of the range do steppe buzzards use the same summering and wintering grounds. Steppe buzzards are slightly gregarious in migration, and travel in variously sized flocks. This race migrates in September to October often from <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape of Africa">Cape of Africa</a> in about a month but does not cross water, following around the <a href="/wiki/Winam_Gulf" title="Winam Gulf">Winam Gulf</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> rather than crossing the several kilometer wide gulf. Similarly, they will funnel along both sides of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. Migratory behavior of steppe buzzards mirrors those of <a href="/wiki/Broad-winged_hawk" title="Broad-winged hawk">broad-winged</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Swainson%27s_hawk" title="Swainson&#39;s hawk">Swainson's hawks</a> (<i>Buteo platypterus</i> &amp; <i>swainsoni</i>) in every significant way as similar long-distance migrating <i>Buteos</i>, including trans-equatorial movements, avoidance of large bodies of waters and flocking behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-Bildstein_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bildstein-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Migrating steppe buzzards will rise up with the morning thermals and can cover an average of hundreds of miles a day using the available currents along mountain ridges and other topographic features.
