The spring migration for steppe buzzards peaks around March–April, but the latest <i>vulpinus</i> arrive in their breeding grounds by late April or early May. Distances covered by migrating steppe buzzards in one way flights from northern Europe (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>) to southern Africa have ranged over 13,000&#160;km (8,100&#160;mi) within a season .<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> For the steppe buzzards from eastern and northern Europe and western Russia (which compromise a majority of all steppe buzzards), peak migratory numbers occur in differing areas in autumn, when the largest recorded movements occurs through <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, than in spring, when the largest recorded movement are to the south in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. The two migratory movements barely differ overall until they reach the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and east Africa, where the largest volume of migrants in autumn occurs at the southern part of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, around <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, while the main volume in spring is in the northernmost strait, around <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. In autumn, numbers of steppe buzzards recorded in migration have ranged up to 32,000 (recorded 1971) in northwestern Turkey (<a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a>) and in northeastern Turkey (Black Sea) up to 205,000 (recorded 1976). Further down in migration, autumn numbers of up to 98,000 have been recorded in passage in <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Between 150,000 and nearly 466,000 Steppe Buzzard have been recorded migrating through Israel during spring, making this not only the most abundant migratory raptor here but one of the largest raptor migrations anywhere in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Spaar_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spaar-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Migratory movements of southern Africa buzzards largely occur along the major mountain ranges, such as the <a href="/wiki/Drakensberg" title="Drakensberg">Drakensberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lebombo_Mountains" title="Lebombo Mountains">Lebombo Mountains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Wintering steppe buzzards occur far more irregularly in <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">Transvaal</a> than <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape region</a> in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The onset of migratory movement for steppe buzzards back to the breeding grounds in southern Africa is mainly in March, peaking in the second week.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Steppe buzzard molt their feathers rapidly upon arrival at wintering grounds and seems to split their flight feather molt between breeding ground in Eurasia and wintering ground in southern Africa, the molt pausing during migration.<sup id="cite_ref-Herremans_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herremans-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In last 50 years, it was recorded that nominate buzzards are typically migrating shorter distances and wintering further north, possibly in response to climate change, resulting in relatively smaller numbers of them at migration sites. They are also extending their breeding range possibly reducing/supplanting steppe buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-Kjellen_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kjellen-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalizations">Vocalizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Vocalizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Resident populations of common buzzards tend to vocalize all year around, whereas migrants tend to vocalize only during the breeding season. Both nominate buzzards and steppe buzzards (and their numerous related subspecies within their types) tend to have similar voices. The main call of the species is a plaintive, far-carrying <i>pee-yow</i> or <i>peee-oo</i>, used as both contact call and more excitedly in aerial displays. Their call is sharper, more ringing when used in aggression, tends to be more drawn-out and wavering when chasing intruders, sharper, more yelping when as warning when approaching the nest or shorter and more explosive when called in alarm. Other variations of their vocal performances include a <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cat</a>-like <i>mew</i>, uttered repeatedly on the wing or when perched, especially in display; a repeated <i>mah</i> has been recorded as uttered by pairs answering each other, further chuckles and croaks have also been recorded at nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fryer_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Juveniles can usually be distinguished by the discordant nature of their calls compared to those of adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Walls_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walls-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg/220px-Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg/330px-Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg/440px-Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1358" data-file-height="918" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heubach_common_buzzard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of a buzzard preying on a rodent.</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard is a generalist predator which hunts a wide variety of prey given the opportunity. Their prey spectrum extents to a wide variety of vertebrates including <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> (from any age from eggs to adult birds), <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> and, rarely, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, as well as to various <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, mostly <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>. Young animals are often attacked, largely the <a href="/wiki/Nidifugous" title="Nidifugous">nidifugous</a> young of various vertebrates. In total well over 300 prey species are known to be taken by common buzzards. Furthermore, prey size can vary from tiny <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a> to large adult <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a> up to nearly twice their body mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Redinov_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redinov-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selas_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Mean body mass of vertebrate prey was estimated at 179.6&#160;g (6.34&#160;oz) in <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sidorovich_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sidorovich-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> At times, they will also subsist partially on carrion, usually of dead mammals or fish.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> However, dietary studies have shown that they mostly prey upon small <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, largely small <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Meheretu2019_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meheretu2019-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Like many temperate zone raptorial birds of varied lineages, <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> are an essential part of the common buzzard's diet. This bird's preference for the interface between woods and open areas frequently puts them in ideal vole habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mebs_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mebs-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Hunting in relatively open areas has been found to increase hunting success whereas more complete shrub cover lowered success.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> A majority of prey is taken by dropping from perch, and is normally taken on ground. Alternately, prey may be hunted in a low flight. This species tends not to hunt in a spectacular stoop but generally drops gently then gradually accelerate at bottom with wings held above the back. Sometimes, the buzzard also forages by random glides or soars over open country, wood edges or clearings. Perch hunting may be done preferentially but buzzards fairly regularly also hunt from a ground position when the habitat demands it. Outside the breeding season, as many 15–30 buzzards have been recorded foraging on ground in a single large field, especially juveniles. Normally the rarest foraging type is hovering. A study from Great Britain indicated that hovering does not seem to increase hunting success.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sim_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sim-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1948" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Buzzard_catched_a_mouse_and_flies_away_with_his_majestic_wings_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A buzzard with a freshly caught rodent, likely a <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">vole</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A high diversity of <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> may be taken given the chance, as around 60 species of rodent have been recorded in the foods of common buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-Redinov_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redinov-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selas_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> It seems clear that <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> are the most significant prey type for European buzzards. Nearly every study from the continent makes reference to the importance, in particular, of the two most numerous and widely distributed European voles: the 28.5&#160;g (1.01&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common vole</a> (<i>Microtus arvalis</i>) and the somewhat more northerly ranging 40&#160;g (1.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Field_vole" title="Field vole">field vole</a> (<i>Microtus agrestis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Halle_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halle-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, field voles were the best represented species in pellets, accounting for 32.1% of 581 pellets.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In southern <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, field voles were again the main food in years with peak vole numbers, accounting for 40.8% of 179 prey items in 1985 and 24.7% of 332 prey items in 1994. Altogether, rodents amount to 67.6% and 58.4% of the foods in these respective peak vole years. However, in low vole population years, the contribution of rodents to the diet was minor.<sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> As far west as the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, common voles were the most regular prey, amounting to 19.6% of 6624 prey items in a very large study.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Common voles were the main foods recorded in central <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, accounting for 26.5% of 606 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The common vole, or other related vole species at times, were the main foods as well in the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> (17.2% of 146 prey items) ranging east to <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Privolshky_Les_Nature_Reserve" title="Privolshky Les Nature Reserve">Privolshky Steppe Nature Reserve</a> (41.8% of 74 prey items) and in <a href="/wiki/Samara_Oblast" title="Samara Oblast">Samara</a> (21.4% of 183 prey items).<sup id="cite_ref-Redinov_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redinov-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Other records from Russia and the Ukraine show voles ranging from slightly secondary prey to as much as 42.2% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, voles, including <i>Microtus</i> species and 18.4&#160;g (0.65&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Bank_vole" title="Bank vole">bank voles</a> (<i>Myodes glareolus</i>), accounted for 34.8% of the biomass on average in 1065 prey items from different study areas over 4 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Sidorovich_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sidorovich-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> At least 12 species of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Microtus" title="Microtus">Microtus</a></i> are known to be hunted by common buzzards and even this is probably conservative, moreover similar species like <a href="/wiki/Lemming" title="Lemming">lemmings</a> will be taken if available.<sup id="cite_ref-Redinov_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redinov-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Costa_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other rodents are taken largely opportunistically rather than by preference. Several <a href="/wiki/Apodemus" title="Apodemus">wood mice</a> (<i>Apodemus ssp.</i>) are known to be taken quite frequently but given their preference for activity in deeper woods than the field-forest interfaces preferred, they are rarely more than secondary food items.<sup id="cite_ref-Halle_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halle-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> An exception was in <a href="/wiki/Samara_Oblast" title="Samara Oblast">Samara</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Yellow-necked_mouse" title="Yellow-necked mouse">yellow-necked mouse</a> (<i>Apodemus flavicollis</i>), one of the largest of its genus at 28.4&#160;g (1.00&#160;oz), made up 20.9%, putting it just behind the common vole in importance.<sup id="cite_ref-Redinov_78-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redinov-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Tree_squirrel" title="Tree squirrel">tree squirrels</a> are readily taken but rarely important in the foods of buzzards in Europe, as buzzards apparently prefer to avoid taking prey from trees nor do they possess the agility typically necessary to capture significant quantities of tree squirrels.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selas_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> All four <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a> that range (mostly) into eastern Europe are also known to be common buzzard prey but little quantitative analysis has gone into how significant such predator-prey relations are.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palomar_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palomar-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Rodent prey taken have ranged in size from the 7.8&#160;g (0.28&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_harvest_mouse" title="Eurasian harvest mouse">Eurasian harvest mouse</a> (<i>Micromys minutus</i>) to the non-native, 1,100&#160;g (2.4&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Muskrat" title="Muskrat">muskrat</a> (<i>Ondatra zibethicus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Steiner_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steiner-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Other rodents taken either seldomly or in areas where the food habits of buzzards are spottily known include <a href="/wiki/Flying_squirrel" title="Flying squirrel">flying squirrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marmot" title="Marmot">marmots</a> (presumably very young if taken alive), <a href="/wiki/Siberian_chipmunk" title="Siberian chipmunk">chipmunks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coypu" title="Coypu">spiny rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blesmol" title="Blesmol">mole-rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cape_gerbil" title="Cape gerbil">gerbils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tristram%27s_jird" title="Tristram&#39;s jird">jirds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Williams%27_jerboa" title="Williams&#39; jerboa">jerboas</a> and occasionally hearty numbers of <a href="/wiki/Dormice" class="mw-redirect" title="Dormice">dormice</a>, although these are nocturnal.<sup id="cite_ref-Herremans_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herremans-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sidorovich_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sidorovich-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pinowski_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinowski-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Surprisingly little research has gone into the diets of wintering steppe buzzards in southern Africa, considering their numerous status there. However, it has been indicated that the main prey remains consist of rodents such as the <a href="/wiki/Four-striped_grass_mouse" title="Four-striped grass mouse">four-striped grass mouse</a> (<i>Rhabdomys pumilio</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Cape_mole-rat" title="Cape mole-rat">Cape mole-rats</a> (<i>Georychus capensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gwynn_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gwynn-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other than rodents, two other groups of mammals can be counted as significant to the diet of common buzzards. One of these main prey type of import in the diets of common buzzards are <a href="/wiki/Leporidae" title="Leporidae">leporids</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lagomorpha" title="Lagomorpha">lagomorphs</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbit</a> (<i>Oryctolagus cuniculus</i>) where it is found in numbers in a wild or feral state. In all dietary studies from Scotland, rabbits were highly important to the buzzard's diet. In southern Scotland, rabbits constituted 40.8% of remains at nests and 21.6% of pellet contents, while lagomorphs (mainly rabbits but also some young <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>) were present in 99% of remains in <a href="/wiki/Moray" title="Moray">Moray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. The nutritional richness relative to the commonest prey elsewhere, such as voles, might account for the high productivity of buzzards here. For example, clutch sizes were twice as large on average where rabbits were common (Moray) than were where they were rare (<a href="/wiki/Glen_Urquhart" title="Glen Urquhart">Glen Urquhart</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swan_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swan-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In northern <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, an area of interest because it is devoid of any native <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">vole</a> species, rabbits were again the main prey. Here, lagomorphs constituted 22.5% of prey items by number and 43.7% by biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Rooney_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> While rabbits are non-native, albeit long-established, in the British Isles, in their native area of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>, rabbits are similarly significant to the buzzard's diet. In <a href="/wiki/Region_of_Murcia" title="Region of Murcia">Murcia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, rabbits were the most common mammal in the diet, making up 16.8% of 167 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In a large study from northeastern Spain, rabbits were dominant in the buzzard's foods, making up 66.5% of 598 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Manosa_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manosa-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, European rabbits were second in number (19.1% of 6624 prey items) only to common voles and the largest contributor of biomass to nests (36.7%).<sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Outside of these (at least historically) rabbit-rich areas, <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">leverets</a> of the common hare species found in Europe can be important supplemental prey. <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hare</a> (<i>Lepus europaeus</i>) were the fourth most important prey species in central <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and the third most significant prey species in <a href="/wiki/Stavropol_Krai" title="Stavropol Krai">Stavropol Krai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pinowski_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinowski-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Buzzards normally attack the young of European rabbits, which as adults can average nearly 2,000&#160;g (4.4&#160;lb), and invariably (so far as is known) only the young of hares, which can average up to twice as massive as rabbits. The mean weights of rabbits taken have various been estimated from 159 to 550&#160;g (5.6 to 19.4&#160;oz) in different areas while <a href="/wiki/Mountain_hare" title="Mountain hare">mountain hares</a> (<i>Lepus timidus</i>) taken in Norway were estimated to average about 1,000&#160;g (2.2&#160;lb), in both cases about a third of the weight of full-grown, prime adults of the respective species. However, hares and rabbits taken by female buzzards can infrequently include specimens that weigh up to 1,600&#160;g (3.5&#160;lb), including at times adult rabbits.<sup id="cite_ref-Selas_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rooney_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reif_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reif-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Francksen_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Francksen-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The other significant mammalian prey type is <a href="/wiki/Eulipotyphla" title="Eulipotyphla">insectivores</a>, among which more than 20 species are known to be taken by this species, including nearly all the species of <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mole_(animal)" title="Mole (animal)">mole</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehog</a> found in Europe. Moles are taken particularly often among this order, since as is the case with "vole-holes", buzzard probably tend to watch molehills in fields for activity and dive quickly from their perch when one of the subterranean mammals pops up.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The most widely found mole in the buzzard's northern range is the 98&#160;g (3.5&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/European_mole" title="European mole">European mole</a> (<i>Talpa europaea</i>) and this is one of the more important non-rodent prey items for the species. This species was present in 55% of 101 remains in <a href="/wiki/Glen_Urquhart" title="Glen Urquhart">Glen Urquhart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and was the second most common prey species (18.6%) in 606 prey items in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bari" title="Bari">Bari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mole" title="Roman mole">Roman mole</a> (<i>Talpa romana</i>), of similar size to the European species, was the leading identified mammalian prey, making up 10.7% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Scillitani_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scillitani-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The full size range of insectivores may be taken by buzzards, ranging from the world's smallest mammal (by weight), the 1.8&#160;g (0.063&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_shrew" title="Etruscan shrew">Etruscan shrew</a> (<i>Suncus etruscus</i>) to arguably the heaviest insectivore, the 800&#160;g (28&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/European_hedgehog" title="European hedgehog">European hedgehog</a> (<i>Erinaceus europaeus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manosa_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manosa-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Mammalian prey for common buzzards other than rodents, insectivores and lagomorphs is rarely taken. Occasionally, some <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a> (including <a href="/wiki/European_polecat" title="European polecat">polecats</a>) and perhaps <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">martens</a> might be attacked by buzzards, more likely the more powerful female buzzard since such prey is potentially dangerous and of similar size to a buzzard itself.<sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Numerous larger mammals, including medium-sized carnivores such as <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">foxes</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a>, are sometimes eaten as carrion by buzzards, mainly during lean winter months. Still-borns of <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> are also visited with some frequency.<sup id="cite_ref-Sidorovich_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sidorovich-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palomar_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palomar-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_(4552169984).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_%284552169984%29.jpg/220px-Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_%284552169984%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_%284552169984%29.jpg/330px-Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_%284552169984%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_%284552169984%29.jpg/440px-Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_%284552169984%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1365" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crow_attacking_Buzzard_-_Woburn_Safari_Park_(4552169984).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">crow</a> mobs a buzzard. Buzzards will readily prey on crows, especially their fledglings.</div></div></div>
<p>When attacking birds, common buzzards chiefly prey on nestlings and fledglings of small to medium-sized birds, largely <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerines</a> but also a variety of <a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">gamebirds</a>, but sometimes also injured, sickly or unwary but healthy adults. While capable of overpowering birds larger than itself, the common buzzard is usually considered to lack the agility necessary to capture many adult birds, even gamebirds which would presumably be weaker fliers considering their relatively heavy bodies and small wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The amount of fledgling and younger birds preyed upon relative to adults is variable, however. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Italian Alps</a>, 72% of birds taken were fledglings or recently fledged juveniles, 19% were nestlings and 8% were adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, in southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, even though the buzzards were taking relatively large bird prey, largely <a href="/wiki/Red_grouse" title="Red grouse">red grouse</a> (<i>Lagopus lagopus scotica</i>), 87% of birds taken were reportedly adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_89-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In total, as in many raptorial birds that are far from bird-hunting specialists, birds are the most diverse group in the buzzard's prey spectrum due to the sheer number and diversity of birds, few raptors do not hunt them at least occasionally. Nearly 150 species of bird have been identified in the common buzzard's diet. In general, despite many that are taken, birds usually take a secondary position in the diet after mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In northern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, birds were fairly numerous in the foods of buzzards. The most often recorded avian prey and 2nd and 3rd most frequent prey species (after only field voles) in <a href="/wiki/Glen_Urquhart" title="Glen Urquhart">Glen Urquhart</a>, were 23.9&#160;g (0.84&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Chaffinch" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaffinch">chaffinch</a> (<i>Fringilla coelebs</i>) and 18.4&#160;g (0.65&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Meadow_pipit" title="Meadow pipit">meadow pipits</a> (<i>Anthus pratensis</i>), with the buzzards taking 195 fledglings of these species against only 90 adults. This differed from <a href="/wiki/Moray" title="Moray">Moray</a> where the most frequent avian prey and 2nd most frequent prey species behind the rabbit was the 480&#160;g (17&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeon</a> (<i>Columba palumbus</i>) and the buzzards took four times as many adults relative to fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Birds were the primary food for common buzzards in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Italian Alps</a>, where they made up 46% of the diet against mammal which accounted for 29% in 146 prey items. The leading prey species here were 103&#160;g (3.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_blackbird" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian blackbird">Eurasian blackbirds</a> (<i>Turdus merula</i>) and 160&#160;g (5.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">Eurasian jays</a> (<i>Garrulus glandarius</i>), albeit largely fledglings were taken of both.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Birds could also take the leading position in years with low vole populations in southern <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a>, namely the blackbird, the 67.7&#160;g (2.39&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Song_thrush" title="Song thrush">song thrush</a> (<i>Turdus philomelos</i>) and the 61&#160;g (2.2&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Redwing" title="Redwing">redwing</a> (<i>Turdus iliacus</i>), which were collectively 22.1% of 244 prey items in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In southern Spain, birds were equal in number to mammals in the diet, both at 38.3%, but most remains were classified as "unidentified medium-sized birds", although the most often identified species of those that apparently could be determined were Eurasian jays and <a href="/wiki/Red-legged_partridge" title="Red-legged partridge">red-legged partridges</a> (<i>Alectoris rufa</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in northern <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, birds were roughly equal in import to mammals but most were unidentified <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rooney_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Seversky_Donets" class="mw-redirect" title="Seversky Donets">Seversky Donets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, birds and mammals both made up 39.3% of the foods of buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Common buzzards may hunt nearly 80 species passerines and nearly all available gamebirds. Like many other largish raptors, gamebirds are attractive to hunt for buzzards due to their ground-dwelling habits. Buzzards were the most frequent predator in a study of juvenile pheasants in England, accounting for 4.3% of 725 deaths (against 3.2% by <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">foxes</a>, 0.7% by <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a> and 0.5% by other mammals).<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> They also prey on a wide size range of birds, ranging down to Europe's smallest bird, the 5.2&#160;g (0.18&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Goldcrest" title="Goldcrest">goldcrest</a> (<i>Regulus regulus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC2_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Very few individual birds hunted by buzzards weigh more than 500&#160;g (1.1&#160;lb). However, there have been some particularly large avian kills by buzzards, including any that weigh more or 1,000&#160;g (2.2&#160;lb), or about the largest average size of a buzzard, have including adults of <a href="/wiki/Mallard" title="Mallard">mallard</a> (<i>Anas platyrhynchos</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_grouse" title="Black grouse">black grouse</a> (<i>Tetrao tetrix</i>), <a href="/wiki/Ring-necked_pheasant" class="mw-redirect" title="Ring-necked pheasant">ring-necked pheasant</a> (<i>Phasianus colchicus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common raven</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>) and some of the larger <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> if ambushed on their nests. The largest avian kill by a buzzard, and possibly largest known overall for the species, was an adult female <a href="/wiki/Western_capercaillie" title="Western capercaillie">western capercaillie</a> (<i>Tetrao urogallus</i>) that weighed an estimated 1,985&#160;g (4.376&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_89-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> At times, buzzards will hunt the young of large birds such as <a href="/wiki/Grey_heron" title="Grey heron">herons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black-necked_crane" title="Black-necked crane">cranes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Other assorted avian prey has included a few species of <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>, most available <a href="/wiki/Columbiformes" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbiformes">pigeons and doves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_cuckoo" title="Common cuckoo">cuckoos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plain_swift" title="Plain swift">swifts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_grebe" title="Little grebe">grebes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rail_(bird)" title="Rail (bird)">rails</a>, nearly 20 assorted <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">shorebirds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Procellariiformes" title="Procellariiformes">tubenoses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_hoopoe" title="Eurasian hoopoe">hoopoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_bee-eater" title="European bee-eater">bee-eaters</a> and several types of <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">woodpecker</a>. Birds with more conspicuous or open nesting areas or habits are more likely to have fledglings or nestlings attacked, such as water birds, while those with more secluded or inaccessible nests, such as pigeons/doves and woodpeckers, adults are more likely to be hunted.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palomar_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palomar-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reptiles_and_amphibians">Reptiles and amphibians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reptiles and amphibians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey,_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey%2C_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg/220px-Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey%2C_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey%2C_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg/330px-Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey%2C_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey%2C_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg/440px-Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey%2C_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buzzard_caught_an_Aesculapian_Snake_but_flew_away_and_lost_his_prey,_so_I_could_take_a_photo_of_the_snake.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A buzzard that caught a large <a href="/wiki/Green_whip_snake" title="Green whip snake">Green whip snake</a> but was flushed from its catch.</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard may be the most regular avian predator of reptiles and amphibians in Europe apart from the sections where they are sympatric with the largely snake-eating <a href="/wiki/Short-toed_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-toed eagle">short-toed eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In total, the prey spectrum of common buzzards include nearly 50 herpetological prey species. In studies from northern and southern Spain, the leading prey numerically were both reptilian, although in <a href="/wiki/Biscay" title="Biscay">Biscay</a> (northern Spain) the leading prey (19%) was classified as "unidentified snakes". In <a href="/wiki/Region_of_Murcia" title="Region of Murcia">Murcia</a>, the most numerous prey was the 77.2&#160;g (2.72&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Ocellated_lizard" title="Ocellated lizard">ocellated lizard</a> (<i>Timon lepidus</i>), at 32.9%. In total, at Biscay and Murcia, reptiles accounted for 30.4% and 35.9% of the prey items, respectively. Findings were similar in a separate study from northeastern Spain, where reptiles amounted to 35.9% of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manosa_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manosa-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bari" title="Bari">Bari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, reptiles were the main prey, making up almost exactly half of the biomass, led by the large <a href="/wiki/Green_whip_snake" title="Green whip snake">green whip snake</a> (<i>Hierophis viridiflavus</i>), maximum size up to 1,360&#160;g (3.00&#160;lb), at 24.2% of food mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Scillitani_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scillitani-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Stavropol_Krai" title="Stavropol Krai">Stavropol Krai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, the 20&#160;g (0.71&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Sand_lizard" title="Sand lizard">sand lizard</a> (<i>Lacerta agilis</i>) was the main prey at 23.7% of 55 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The 16&#160;g (0.56&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Anguis_fragilis" title="Anguis fragilis">slowworm</a> (<i>Anguis fragilis</i>), a legless lizard, became the most numerous prey for the buzzards of southern Norway in low vole years, amounting to 21.3% of 244 prey items in 1993 and were also common even in the peak vole year of 1994 (19% of 332 prey items).<sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> More or less any snake in Europe is potential prey and the buzzard has been known to be uncharacteristically bold in going after and overpowering large snakes such as <a href="/wiki/Elaphe" title="Elaphe">rat snakes</a>, ranging up to nearly 1.5&#160;m (4&#160;ft 11&#160;in) in length, and healthy, large <a href="/wiki/Vipera" title="Vipera">vipers</a> despite the danger of being struck by such prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Selas2_87-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> However, in at least one case, the corpse of a female buzzard was found envenomed over the body of an <a href="/wiki/European_adder" class="mw-redirect" title="European adder">adder</a> that it had killed.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> In some parts of range, the common buzzard acquires the habit of taking many frogs and toads.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> This was the case in the <a href="/wiki/Mogilev_Region" title="Mogilev Region">Mogilev Region</a> of <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> where the 23&#160;g (0.81&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Moor_frog" title="Moor frog">moor frog</a> (<i>Rana arvalis</i>) was the major prey (28.5%) over several years, followed by other frogs and toads amounting to 39.4% of the diet over the years.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernichko_93-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernichko-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In central <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the 46&#160;g (1.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_toad" title="Common toad">common toad</a> (<i>Bufo bufo</i>) was the most numerous prey species, accounting for 21.7% of 263 prey items, while the <a href="/wiki/Common_frog" title="Common frog">common frog</a> (<i>Rana temporaria</i>) made up a further 14.7% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Swan_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swan-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Frogs made up about 10% of the diet in central <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinowski_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinowski-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates_and_other_prey">Invertebrates and other prey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Invertebrates and other prey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When common buzzards feed on invertebrates, these are chiefly <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a> in Europe and largely seemed to be preyed on by juvenile buzzards with less refined hunting skills or in areas with mild winters and ample swarming or social insects. In most dietary studies, invertebrates are at best a minor supplemental contributor to the buzzard's diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, roughly a dozen beetle species have found in the foods of buzzards from the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Redinov_78-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redinov-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In winter in northeastern Spain, it was found that the buzzards switched largely from the vertebrate prey typically taken during spring and summer to a largely <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insect</a>-based diet. Most of this prey was unidentified but the most frequently identified were <a href="/wiki/European_mantis" title="European mantis">European mantis</a> (<i>Mantis religiosa</i>) and <a href="/wiki/European_mole_cricket" class="mw-redirect" title="European mole cricket">European mole cricket</a> (<i>Gryllotalpa gryllotalpa</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Manosa_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manosa-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, 30.8% of the food by number was found to be <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Redinov_78-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redinov-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Especially in winter quarters such as southern Africa, common buzzards are often attracted to swarming <a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locusts</a> and other orthopterans.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In this way the steppe buzzard may mirror a similar long-distance migrant from the Americas, the <a href="/wiki/Swainson%27s_hawk" title="Swainson&#39;s hawk">Swainson's hawk</a>, which feeds its young largely on nutritious vertebrates but switches to a largely insect-based once the reach their distant wintering grounds in <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bildstein_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bildstein-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eritea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Eritea (page does not exist)">Eritea</a>, 18 returning migrant steppe buzzards were seen to feed together on swarms of <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> For wintering steppe buzzards in <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, one source went so far as to refer to them as primarily insectivorous, apparently being somewhat locally specialized to feeding on <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Stomach contents in buzzards from <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> apparently consisted largely of <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a> (alternately with <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">Fish</a> tend to be the rarest class of prey found in the common buzzard's foods. There are a couple cases of predation of fish detected in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, while elsewhere they've been known to have fed upon <a href="/wiki/European_eel" title="European eel">eels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">carp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_predatory_relationships">Interspecies predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg/220px-White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg/330px-White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg/440px-White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagle</a> being <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mobbed</a> by a pair of common buzzards over the <a href="/wiki/Canna,_Scotland" title="Canna, Scotland">Isle of Canna</a>, as the eagle will sometimes prey on the buzzard.</div></div></div>
<p>Common buzzards co-occur with dozens of other raptorial birds through their breeding, resident and wintering grounds. There may be many other birds that broadly overlap in prey selection to some extent. Furthermore, their preference for interferences of forest and field is used heavily by many birds of prey. Some of the most similar species by diet are the <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> (<i>Falco tinniculus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Hen_harrier" title="Hen harrier">hen harrier</a> (<i>Circus cyaenus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>), not to mention nearly every European species of owl, as all but two may locally prefer <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> in their diets.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bergman_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergman-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Diet overlap was found to be extensive between buzzards and <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, with 61.9% of prey selection overlapping by species although the dietary breadth of the fox was broader and more opportunistic. Both fox dens and buzzard roosts were found to be significantly closer to high vole areas relative to the overall environment here.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The only other widely found European <i>Buteo</i>, the rough-legged buzzard, comes to winter extensively with common buzzards. It was found in southern Sweden, habitat, hunting and prey selection often overlapped considerably. Rough-legged buzzards appear to prefer slightly more open habitat and took slightly fewer <a href="/wiki/Apodemus" title="Apodemus">wood mice</a> than common buzzard. Roughlegs also hover much more frequently and are more given to hunting in high winds. The two buzzards are aggressive towards one another and excluded each other from winter feeding territories in similar ways to the way they exclude conspecifics. In northern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the buffer of their habitat preferences apparently accounted for the lack of effect on each other's occupancy between the two buzzard species.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schindler_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schindler-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Despite a broad range of overlap, very little is known about the ecology of common and <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzards</a> where they co-exist. However, it can be inferred from the long-legged species preference for predation on differing prey, such as <a href="/wiki/Spalax" title="Spalax">blind mole-rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meriones_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Meriones (genus)">gerbils</a>, from the voles usually preferred by the common species, that serious competition for food is unlikely.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A more direct negative effect has been found in buzzard's co-existence with <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gentilis</i>). Despite the considerable discrepancy of the two species dietary habits, habitat selection in Europe is largely similar between buzzards and goshawks. Goshawks are slightly larger than buzzards and are more powerful, agile and generally more aggressive birds, and so they are considered dominant. In studies from Germany and Sweden, buzzards were found to be less disturbance sensitive than goshawks but were probably displaced into inferior nesting spots by the dominant goshawks. The exposure of buzzards to a dummy goshawk was found to decrease breeding success whereas there was no effect on breeding goshawks when they were exposed to a dummy buzzard.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In many cases, in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, goshawks displaced buzzards from their nests to take them over for themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hakkarainen_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hakkarainen-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, buzzards productivity was correlated to prey population variations, particularly voles which could vary from 10–80 per hectare, whereas goshawks were seemingly unaffected by prey variations; buzzards were found here to number 1.73 pair per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi) against goshawk 1.63 pair per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, the slightly larger counterpart of buzzards in North America, the <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a> (which is also slightly larger than American goshawks, the latter averaging smaller than European ones) are more similar in diet to goshawks there. Redtails are not invariably dominated by goshawks and are frequently able to outcompete them by virtue of greater dietary and habitat flexibility. Furthermore, red-tailed hawks are apparently equally capable of killing goshawks as goshawks are of killing them (killings are more one-sided in buzzard-goshawk interactions in favour of the latter).<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Other raptorial birds, including many of similar or mildly larger size than common buzzards themselves, may dominate or displace the buzzard, especially with aims to take over their nests. Species such as the <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>), <a href="/wiki/Booted_eagle" title="Booted eagle">booted eagle</a> (<i>Hieraeetus pennatus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a> have been known to displace actively nesting buzzards, although in some cases the buzzards may attempt to defend themselves. The broad range of accipitrids that take over buzzard nests is somewhat unusual. More typically, common buzzards are victims of nest parasitism to <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a>, as neither of these other kinds of raptorial birds builds their own nests, but these may regularly take up occupancy on already abandoned or alternate nests rather than ones the buzzards are actively using.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Costa_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Even with birds not traditionally considered raptorial, such as <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a>, may compete for nesting sites with buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore,_N._W._1957_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore,_N._W._1957-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Despite often being dominated in nesting site confrontations by even similarly sized raptors, buzzards appear to be bolder in direct competition over food with other raptors outside of the context of breeding, and has even been known to displace larger birds of prey such as <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kites</a> (<i>Milvus milvus</i>) and female buzzards may also dominate male goshawks (which are much smaller than the female goshawk) at disputed kills.<sup id="cite_ref-Hakkarainen_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hakkarainen-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg/220px-Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg/330px-Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg/440px-Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rupfung_eines_M%C3%A4usebussard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The remains of a common buzzard that was preyed on by a <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Common buzzards are occasionally threatened by predation by other raptorial birds. Northern goshawks have been known to have preyed upon buzzards in a few cases.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio2_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio2-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bjorklund_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bjorklund-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Much larger raptors are known to have killed a few buzzards as well, including <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagles</a> (<i>Aquila nipalensis</i>) on migrating steppe buzzards in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. Further instances of predation on buzzards have involved <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bonelli%27s_eagle" title="Bonelli&#39;s eagle">Bonelli's</a> (<i>Aquila fasciata</i>) and <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a> (<i>Haliaeetus albicilla</i>) in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Besides preying on adult buzzard, white-tailed eagles have been known to raise buzzards with their own young. These are most likely cases of eagles carrying off young buzzard nestlings with the intention of predation but, for unclear reasons, not killing them. Instead the mother eagle comes to brood the young buzzard. Despite the difference of the two species diets, white-tailed eagles are surprisingly successful at raising young buzzards (which are conspicuously much smaller than their own nestlings) to fledging.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The most serious predator of common buzzards, however, is almost certainly the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>). This is a very large owl with a mean body mass about three to four times greater than that of a buzzard. The eagle-owl, despite often taking small mammals that broadly overlap with those selected by buzzards, is considered a "super-predator" that is a major threat to nearly all co-existing raptorial birds, capably destroying whole broods of other raptorial birds and dispatching adult raptors even as large as eagles. Due to their large numbers in edge habitats, common buzzards frequently feature heavily in the eagle-owl's diet. Eagle-owls, as will some other large owls, also readily expropriate the nests of buzzards.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio2_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio2-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, the buzzard was the third and fifth most frequent prey species for eagle-owls, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bayle_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayle-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The reintroduction of eagle-owls to sections of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> has been found to have a slight deleterious effect on the local occupancy of common buzzards. The only sparing factor is the temporal difference (the buzzard nesting later in the year than the eagle-owl) and buzzards may locally be able to avoid nesting near an active eagle-owl family.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> As the ecology of the wintering population is relatively little studied, a similar very large owl at the top of the avian food chain, the <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle-owl" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle-owl">Verreaux's eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo lacteus</i>), is the only known predator of wintering steppe buzzards in southern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Despite not being known predators of buzzards, other large, vole-eating owls are known to displace or to be avoided by nesting buzzards, such as <a href="/wiki/Great_grey_owl" title="Great grey owl">great grey owls</a> (<i>Strix nebulosa</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owls</a> (<i>Strix uralensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Bjorklund_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bjorklund-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Unlike with large birds of prey, next to nothing is known of mammalian predators of common buzzards, despite up to several nestlings and fledglings being likely depredated by mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Austin_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tubbs_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tubbs-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common buzzards themselves rarely present a threat to other raptorial birds but may occasionally kill a few of those of smaller size. The buzzard is a known predator of 237&#160;g (8.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawks</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>), 184&#160;g (6.5&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> and 152&#160;g (5.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Lesser_kestrel" title="Lesser kestrel">lesser kestrel</a> (<i>Falco naumanni</i>) .<sup id="cite_ref-Steiner_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steiner-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps surprisingly, given the nocturnal habits of this prey, the group of raptorial birds the buzzard is known to hunt most extensively is owls.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Known owl prey has included 419&#160;g (14.8&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>), 92&#160;g (3.2&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/European_scops_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="European scops owl">European scops owls</a> (<i>Otus scops</i>), 475&#160;g (16.8&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owls</a> (<i>Strix alucco</i>), 169&#160;g (6.0&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owls</a> (<i>Athene noctua</i>), 138&#160;g (4.9&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Boreal_owl" title="Boreal owl">boreal owls</a> (<i>Aegolius funereus</i>), 286&#160;g (10.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owls</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>) and 355&#160;g (12.5&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar_92-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manosa_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manosa-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steiner_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steiner-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Despite their relatively large size, tawny owls are known to avoid buzzards as there are several records of them preying upon the owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_91-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_%281%29.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_%281%29.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_%281%29.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="552" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_-Scotland_-pair-8_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of common buzzards in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting_territories_and_density">Nesting territories and density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nesting territories and density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
