<p>Home ranges of common buzzards are generally 0.5 to 2&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (0.19 to 0.77&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The size of breeding territory seem to be generally correlated with food supply.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kruger_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruger-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In a German study, the range was 0.8 to 1.8&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (0.31 to 0.69&#160;sq&#160;mi) with an average of 1.26&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (0.49&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Schindler_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schindler-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Some of the lowest pair densities of common buzzards seem to come from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. For instance, in <a href="/wiki/Kerzhenets_Nature_Reserve" title="Kerzhenets Nature Reserve">Kerzhenets Nature Reserve</a>, the recorded density was 0.6 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) and the average distance of nearest neighbors was 3.8&#160;km (2.4&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Snowdonia" title="Snowdonia">Snowdonia</a> region of northern <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> held a pair per 9.7&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.7&#160;sq&#160;mi) with a mean nearest neighbor distance of 1.95&#160;km (1.21&#160;mi); in adjacent <a href="/wiki/Migneint" title="Migneint">Migneint</a>, pair occurrence was 7.2&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (2.8&#160;sq&#160;mi), with a mean distance of 1.53&#160;km (0.95&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Macizo_de_Teno" title="Macizo de Teno">Teno massif</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, the average density was estimated as 23 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi), similar to that of a middling continental population.<sup id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> On another set of islands, on <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> the density of pairs was lower at 5.7 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi); here buzzards tend to have an irregular distribution, some in lower intensity harvest olive groves but their occurrence actually more common in agricultural than natural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In the Italian <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, it was recorded in 1993–96 that there were from 28 to 30 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_118-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In central Italy, density average was lower at 19.74 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Higher density areas are known than those above. Two areas of <a href="/wiki/The_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Midlands">the Midlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> showed occupancies of 81 and 22 territorial pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). High buzzard densities there were associated with high proportions of unimproved pasture and mature woodland within the estimated territories.<sup id="cite_ref-Sim_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sim-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Similarly high densities of common buzzards were estimated in central <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> using two different methods, here indicating densities of 96 to 129 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Sotnar2_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sotnar2-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Despite claims from the study of the English midlands were the highest known territory density for the species, a number ranging from 32 to 51 pairs in wooded area of merely 22&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (8.5&#160;sq&#160;mi) in <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> seems to surely exceed even those densities. The Czech study hypothesized that fragmentation of forest in human management of lands for <a href="/wiki/Mouflon" title="Mouflon">wild sheep</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>, creating exceptional concentrations of prey such as voles, and lack of appropriate habitat in surrounding regions for the exceptionally high density.<sup id="cite_ref-Sim_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sim-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_(553474414).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Buteo_buteo_%28553474414%29.jpg/220px-Buteo_buteo_%28553474414%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Buteo_buteo_%28553474414%29.jpg/330px-Buteo_buteo_%28553474414%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Buteo_buteo_%28553474414%29.jpg/440px-Buteo_buteo_%28553474414%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1288" data-file-height="955" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_buteo_(553474414).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A territorial dogfight between three buzzards in the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Common buzzards maintain their territories through flight displays. In Europe, territorial behaviour generally starts in February. However, displays are not uncommon throughout year in resident pairs, especially by males, and can elicit similar displays by neighbors.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In them, common buzzards generally engage in high circling, spiraling upward on slightly raised wings. Mutual high circling by pairs sometimes go on at length, especially during the period prior to or during breeding season. In mutual displays, a pair may follow each other at 10–50&#160;m (33–164&#160;ft) in level flight. During the mutual displays, the male may engage in exaggerated deep flapping or zig-zag tumbling, apparently in response to the female being too distant. Two or three pairs may circle together at times and as many as 14 individual adults have been recorded over established display sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Sky-dancing by common buzzards have been recorded in spring and autumn, typically by male but sometimes by female, nearly always with much calling. Their sky-dances are of the rollercoaster type, with upward sweep until they start to stall, but sometimes embellished with loops or rolls at the top. Next in the sky-dance, they dive on more or less closed wings before spreading them and shooting up again, upward sweeps of up to 30&#160;m (98&#160;ft), with dive drops of up to at least 60&#160;m (200&#160;ft). These dances may be repeated in series of 10 to 20. In the climax of the sky dance, the undulations become progressive shallower, often slowing and terminating directly onto a perch. Various other aerial displays include low contour flight or weaving among trees, frequently with deep beats and exaggerated upstrokes which show underwing pattern to rivals perched below.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Talon grappling and occasionally cartwheeling downward with feet interlocked has been recorded in buzzards and, as in many raptors, is likely the physical culmination of the aggressive territorial display, especially between males.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Despite the highly territorial nature of buzzards and their devotion to a single mate and breeding ground each summer, there is one case of a <a href="/wiki/Polyandrous" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyandrous">polyandrous</a> trio of buzzards nesting in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
In North-Estonian Neeruti landscape reserve (area 1272 ha) found in years 1989 and 1990 Marek Vahula 9 populated nest. This is sovereign public density of population. One nest founded in 12.06.1982 and this is apparently oldest nest of Common Buzzard, what is populated until today.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nests">Nests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Common buzzards tend to build a bulky nest of sticks, twigs and often <a href="/wiki/Calluna" title="Calluna">heather</a>. Commonly, nests are up to 1 to 1.2&#160;m (3&#160;ft 3&#160;in to 3&#160;ft 11&#160;in) across and 60&#160;cm (24&#160;in) deep. With reuse over years, the diameter can reach or exceed 1.5&#160;m (4&#160;ft 11&#160;in) and weight of nests can reach over 200&#160;kg (440&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Active nests tend to be lined with greenery, most often this consists of broad-leafed foliage but sometimes also includes <a href="/wiki/Juncaceae" title="Juncaceae">rush</a> or <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a> locally. Nest height in trees is commonly 3 to 25&#160;m (9.8 to 82.0&#160;ft), usually by main trunk or main crutch of the tree.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, trees used for nesting consisted mostly of <a href="/wiki/Fagus_sylvatica" title="Fagus sylvatica">red beeches</a> (<i>Fagus sylvatica</i>) (in 337 cases), whereas a further 84 were in assorted <a href="/wiki/Quercus" class="mw-redirect" title="Quercus">oaks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Buzzards were recorded to nest almost exclusively in <a href="/wiki/Pinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinus">pines</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> at a mean height of 14.5&#160;m (48&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Trees are generally used for a nesting location but they will also utilize crags or bluffs if trees are unavailable.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Buzzards in one English study were surprisingly partial to nesting on well-vegetated banks and due to the rich surrounding environment habitat and prey population, were actually more productive than nests located in other locations here.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, a few ground nests were recorded in high prey-level agricultural areas in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> In the Italian <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, 81% of 108 nests were on cliffs.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_118-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The common buzzard generally lacks the propensity of its Nearctic counterpart, the <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a>, to occasionally nest on or near manmade structures (often in heavily urbanized areas) but in Spain some pairs recorded nesting along the perimeter of abandoned buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Pairs often have several nests but some pairs may use one over several consecutive years. Two to four alternate nests in a territory is typical for common buzzards, especially those breeding further north in their range.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selas3_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas3-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_eggs">Reproduction and eggs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reproduction and eggs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_(8263824243).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%288263824243%29.jpg/220px-The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%288263824243%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="312" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%288263824243%29.jpg/330px-The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%288263824243%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%288263824243%29.jpg/440px-The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%288263824243%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1986" data-file-height="2817" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_(8263824243).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The much plainer egg of the common buzzard contrasted with that of the <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">European honey buzzard</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The breeding season commences at differing times based on latitude. Common buzzard breeding seasons may fall as early as January to April but typically the breeding season is March to July in much of <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a>. In the northern stretches of the range the breeding season may last into May–August. Mating usually occurs on or near the nest and lasts about 15 seconds, typically occurring several times a day.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Eggs are usually laid in 2 to 3-day intervals. The clutch size can range from to 2 to 6, a relatively large clutch for an accipitrid. More northerly and westerly buzzard usually bear larger clutches, which average nearer 3, than those further east and south.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, the average clutch size is about 2 to 2.3.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> From 4 locations in different parts of Europe, 43% had clutch size of 2, 41% had size of 3, clutches of 1 and 4 each constituted about 8%.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Laying dates are remarkably constant throughout <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>. There are, however, highly significant differences in clutch size between British study areas. These do not follow any latitudinal gradient and it is likely that local factors such as habitat and prey availability are more important determinants of clutch size.<sup id="cite_ref-Austin_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are white in ground colour, rather round in shape with sporadic red to brown markings sometimes lightly showing. In the nominate race, egg size is 49.8–63.8&#160;mm (1.96–2.51&#160;in) in height by 39.1–48.2&#160;mm (1.54–1.90&#160;in) in diameter with an average of 55&#160;mm ×&#160;44&#160;mm (2.2&#160;in ×&#160;1.7&#160;in) in 600 eggs. In the race of <i>vulpinus</i>, egg height is 48–63&#160;mm (1.9–2.5&#160;in) by 39.2–47.5&#160;mm (1.54–1.87&#160;in) with an average of 54.2&#160;mm ×&#160;42.8&#160;mm (2.13&#160;in ×&#160;1.69&#160;in) in 303 eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Eggs are generally laid in late March to early April in extreme south, sometime in April in most of Europe, into May and possibly even early June in the extreme north. If eggs are lost to a predator (including humans) or fail in some other way, common buzzards do not usually lay replacement clutches but they have been recorded, even with 3 attempts of clutches by a single female.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swann_108-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The females does most but not all of the incubating, doing so for a total of 33–35 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The female remains at the nest brooding the young in the early stages with the male bringing all prey. At about 8–12 days, both the male and female will bring prey but female continues to do all feeding until the young can tear up their own prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_young">Development of young</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Development of young">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG/220px-Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG/330px-Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG/440px-Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3030" data-file-height="2286" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buteo_nestling_juedischer_Friedhof.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two young buzzard nestlings.</div></div></div>
<p>Once hatching commences, it may take 48 hours for the chick to chip out. Hatching may take place over 3–7 days, with new hatchlings averaging about 45&#160;g (1.6&#160;oz) in body mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Often the youngest nestling dies from starvation, especially in broods of three or more.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In nestlings, the first down replaces by longer, coarser down at about 7 days of age with the first proper feathers appearing at 12 to 15 days. The young are nearly fully feathered rather than downy at about a month of age and can start to feed themselves as well. The first attempts to leave the nest are often at about 40–50 days, averaging usually 40–45 in nominate buzzards in Europe, but more quickly on average at 40–42 in <i>vulpinus</i>. Fledging occurs typically at 43–54 days but in extreme cases at as late 62 days. Sexual dimorphism is apparent in European fledglings, as females often scale about 1,000&#160;g (2.2&#160;lb) against 780&#160;g (1.72&#160;lb) in males.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tyack_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyack-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> After leaving the nest, buzzards generally stay close by, but with migratory ones there is more definitive movement generally southbound. Full independence is generally sought 6 to 8 weeks after fledging. 1st year birds generally remain in wintering area for following summer but then return to near area of origin but then migrate south again without breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Radio-tracking suggests that most dispersal, even relatively early dispersals, by juvenile buzzards is undertaken independently rather than via exile by parents, as has been recorded in some other birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> In common buzzards, generally speaking, siblings stay quite close to each other after dispersal from their parents and form something of a social group, although parents usually tolerate their presence on their territory until they are laying another clutch. However, the social group of siblings disbands at about a year of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Tyack_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyack-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Walls_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walls-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Juvenile buzzards are subordinate to adults during most encounters and tend to avoid direct confrontations and actively defended territories until they are of appropriate age (usually at least 2 years of age).<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This was the case as well for steppe buzzard juveniles wintering in southern Africa, although in some cases juveniles were able to successfully <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">steal prey</a> from adults there.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg/220px-Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg/330px-Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg/440px-Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2106" data-file-height="1404" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Buzzard_fledgling_in_Pinus_nigra_2010-06-16.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A common buzzard recent fledgling in a pine tree.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_success_rates">Breeding success rates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Breeding success rates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Numerous factors may weigh into the breeding success of common buzzards. Chiefly among these are prey populations, habitat, disturbance and persecution levels and innerspecies competition.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In Germany, intra- and interspecific competition, plumage morph, laying date, precipitation levels and anthropogenic disturbances in the breeding territory, in declining order, were deemed to be the most significant bearers of breeding success.<sup id="cite_ref-Kruger_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruger-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In an accompanying study, it was found that a mere 17% of adult birds of both sexes present in a German study area produced 50% of offspring, so breeding success may be lower than perceived and many adult buzzards for unknown causes may not attempt to breed at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Kruger2_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruger2-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> High breeding success was detected in <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, due likely to hearty prey populations (rabbits) but also probably a lower local rate of persecution than elsewhere in the British isles. Here, the mean number of fledglings were 1.75 against 0.82–1.41 in other parts of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Austin_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> It was found in the English <a href="/wiki/The_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Midlands">Midlands</a> that breeding success both by measure of clutch size and mean number of fledglings, was relatively high thanks again to high prey populations. Breeding success was lower farther from significant stands of trees in the Midlands and most nesting failures that could be determined occurred in the incubation stage, possibly in correlation with predation of eggs by <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sim_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sim-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> More significant than even prey, late winter-early spring was found to be likely the primary driver of breeding success in buzzards from southern <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>. Here, even in peak <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">vole</a> years, nesting success could be considerably hampered by heavy snow at this crucial stage. In Norway, large clutches of 3+ were expected only in years with minimal snow cover, high vole populations and lighter rains in May–June.<sup id="cite_ref-Selas3_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selas3-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> In the Italian <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, the mean number of fledglings per pair was 1.07.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_118-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> 33.4% of nesting attempts were failures per a study in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, with an average of 1.06 of all nesting attempts and 1.61 for all successful attempt. In Germany, weather conditions and rodent populations seemed to be the primary drivers of nesting success.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Region_of_Murcia" title="Region of Murcia">Murcia</a> part of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Biscay" title="Biscay">Biscay</a> to the north, higher levels of interspecific competition from <a href="/wiki/Booted_eagle" title="Booted eagle">booted eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawks</a> did not appear to negatively affect breeding success due to more ample prey populations (rabbits again) in Murcia than in Biscay.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_80-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Westphalia" title="Westphalia">Westphalia</a> area of Germany, it was found that intermediate colour morphs were more productive than those that were darker or lighter.<sup id="cite_ref-Kruger2_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruger2-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> For reasons that are not entirely clear, apparently fewer parasites were found to afflict broods of intermediate plumaged buzzard less so than dark and light <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypes</a>, in particular higher <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a> levels somehow were found to be more inviting to parasitic organism that effect the health of the buzzard's offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The composition of habitat and its relation to human disturbance were important variables for the dark and light phenotypes but were less important to intermediate individuals. Thus selection pressures resulting from different factors did not vary much between sexes but varied between the three phenotypes in the population.<sup id="cite_ref-Kruger3_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruger3-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Breeding success in areas with wild <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbits</a> was considerably effected by <a href="/wiki/Myxomatosis" title="Myxomatosis">rabbit myxomatosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rabbit_haemorrhagic_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbit haemorrhagic disease">rabbit haemorrhagic disease</a>, both of which have heavily depleted wild rabbit population. Breeding success in formerly rabbit-rich areas were recorded to decrease from as much as 2.6 to as little as 0.9 young per pair.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore,_N._W._1957_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore,_N._W._1957-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Age of first breeding in several radio-tagged buzzards showed only a single male breeding as early as his 2nd summer (at about a year of age). Significantly more buzzards were found to start breeding at the 3 summer but breeding attempts can be individually erratic given the availability of habitat, food and mates.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> The mean life expectancy was estimated at 6.3 years in the late 1950s, but this was at a time of high persecution when humans were causing 50–80% of buzzard deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-Mebs_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mebs-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In a more modern context with regionally reduced persecution rates, the lifespan expected can be higher (possibly in excess of 10 years at times) but is still widely variable due to a wide variety of factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Kruger3_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kruger3-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_buzzard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Buzzard_(Steppe_Buzzard),_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus,_on_a_fence_along_the_R42,_Gauteng,_South_Africa_(32070091223).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Common_Buzzard_%28Steppe_Buzzard%29%2C_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus%2C_on_a_fence_along_the_R42%2C_Gauteng%2C_South_Africa_%2832070091223%29.jpg/220px-Common_Buzzard_%28Steppe_Buzzard%29%2C_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus%2C_on_a_fence_along_the_R42%2C_Gauteng%2C_South_Africa_%2832070091223%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Common_Buzzard_%28Steppe_Buzzard%29%2C_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus%2C_on_a_fence_along_the_R42%2C_Gauteng%2C_South_Africa_%2832070091223%29.jpg/330px-Common_Buzzard_%28Steppe_Buzzard%29%2C_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus%2C_on_a_fence_along_the_R42%2C_Gauteng%2C_South_Africa_%2832070091223%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Common_Buzzard_%28Steppe_Buzzard%29%2C_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus%2C_on_a_fence_along_the_R42%2C_Gauteng%2C_South_Africa_%2832070091223%29.jpg/440px-Common_Buzzard_%28Steppe_Buzzard%29%2C_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus%2C_on_a_fence_along_the_R42%2C_Gauteng%2C_South_Africa_%2832070091223%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4247" data-file-height="2729" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Buzzard_(Steppe_Buzzard),_Buteo_buteo_vulpinus,_on_a_fence_along_the_R42,_Gauteng,_South_Africa_(32070091223).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wintering steppe buzzard in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The common buzzard is one of the most numerous birds of prey in its range. Almost certainly, it is the most numerous diurnal bird of prey throughout Europe. Conservative estimates put the total population at no fewer than 700,000 pairs in Europe, which are more than twice the total estimates for the next four birds of prey estimated as most common: the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (more than 340,000 pairs), the <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> (more than 330,000 pairs) and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (more than 160,000 pairs).<sup id="cite_ref-BirdlifeEurope_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdlifeEurope-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Ferguson-Lees et al. roughly estimated that the total population of the common buzzard ranges to nearly 5 million pairs but at time was including the now spilit-off species of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_buzzard" title="Eastern buzzard">eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_buzzard" title="Himalayan buzzard">Himalayan buzzards</a> in those numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> These numbers may be excessive but the total population of common buzzards is certain to total well over seven figures.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> More recently, the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> estimated the common buzzard species (sans the Himalayan and eastern species) to number somewhere between 2.1 and 3.7 million birds, which would put this buzzard one of the most numerous of all acciptrid family members (estimates for Eurasian sparrowhawks, red-tailed hawks and northern goshawks also may range over 2 million).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In 1991, other than their absence in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, after having been extent as breeder by 1910, buzzards recolonized <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> sometime in the 1950s and has increased by the 1990s to 26 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rooney_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Supplemental feeding has reportedly helped the Irish buzzard population to rebound, especially where rabbits have decreased.<sup id="cite_ref-Rooney2_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Most other countries have at least four figures of breeding pairs. As of the 1990s, other countries such as <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> all numbered pairs well into five figures, while <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> had an estimated 140,000 pairs and European Russian may have held 500,000 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BirdlifeEurope_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdlifeEurope-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Between 44,000 and 61,000 pairs nested in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> by 2001 with numbers gradually increasing after past persecution, habitat alteration and prey reductions, making it by far the most abundant diurnal raptor there.<sup id="cite_ref-Clements_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clements-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Westphalia" title="Westphalia">Westphalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, population of Buzzards was shown to nearly triple over the last few decades. The Westphalian buzzards are possibly benefiting from increasingly warmer mean climate, which in turn is increasing vulnerability of voles. However, the rate of increase was significantly greater in males than in females, in part because of reintroduced <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owls</a> to the region preying on nests (including the brooding mother), which may in turn put undue pressure on the local buzzard population.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At least 238 common buzzards killed through persecution were recovered in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> from 1975 to 1989, largely through poisoning. Persecution did not significantly differ at any time due this span of years nor did the persecution rates decrease, nor did it when compared to rates of last survey of this in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> While some persecution persists in England, it is probably slightly less common today.<sup id="cite_ref-Clements_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clements-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> The buzzard was found to be the most vulnerable raptor to power-line collision fatalities in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> probably as it is one of the most common largish birds, and together with the <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common raven</a>, it accounted for nearly a third of recorded electrocutions.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Given its relative abundance, the common buzzard is held as an ideal <a href="/wiki/Bioindicator" title="Bioindicator">bioindicator</a>, as they are effected by a range of pesticide and metal contamination through pollution like other raptors but are largely resilient to these at the population levels. In turn, this allows biologists to study (and <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvest</a> as necessary) the buzzards intensively and their environments without affecting their overall population. The lack of affect may be due to the buzzard's adaptability as well as it relatively short, terrestrially based food chain, which exposes them to less risk of contamination and population depletions than raptors that prey more heavily on water-based prey (such as some large eagles) or birds (such as <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Common buzzards are seldom vulnerable to egg-shell thinning from <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> as are other raptors but egg-shell thinning has been recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Other factors that negatively effect raptors have been studied in common buzzards are <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_worms" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasitic worms">helminths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avipoxvirus" title="Avipoxvirus">avipoxvirus</a> and assorted other <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Bildstein-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Bildstein_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bildstein_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bildstein_3-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Bildstein, K. L., &amp; Zalles, J. I. (2005). <i>Old World versus New World long-distance migration in accipiters, buteos, and falcons</i>. Birds of two worlds: the ecology and evolution of migration.
